If a copy of the MPL was not distributed with this\n     5→ * file, You can obtain one at http://mozilla.org/MPL/2.0/. */\n     6→\n     7→/* JS parser. */\n     8→\n     9→#ifndef frontend_Parser_h\n    10→#define frontend_Parser_h\n    11→\n    12→/*\n    13→ * [SMDOC] JS Parser\n    14→ *\n    15→ * JS parsers capable of generating ASTs from source text.\n    16→ *\n    17→ * A parser embeds token stream information, then gets and matches tokens to\n    18→ * generate a syntax tree that, if desired, BytecodeEmitter will use to compile\n    19→ * bytecode.\n    20→ *\n    21→ * Like token streams (see the comment near the top of TokenStream.h), parser\n    22→ * classes are heavily templatized -- along the token stream's character-type\n    23→ * axis, and also along a full-parse/syntax-parse axis.  Certain limitations of\n    24→ * C++ (primarily the inability to partially specialize function templates),\n    25→ * plus the desire to minimize compiled code size in duplicate function\n    26→ * template instantiations wherever possible, mean that Parser exhibits much of\n    27→ * the same unholy template/inheritance complexity as token streams.\n    28→ *\n    29→ * == ParserSharedBase ==\n    30→ *\n    31→ * ParserSharedBase is the base class for both regular JS and BinAST parsing.\n    32→ * This class contains common fields and methods between both parsers. There is\n    33→ * currently no BinAST parser here so this can potentially be merged into the\n    34→ * ParserBase type below.\n    35→ *\n    36→ * == ParserBase → ParserSharedBase, ErrorReportMixin ==\n    37→ *\n    38→ * ParserBase is the base class for regular JS parser, shared by all regular JS\n    39→ * parsers of all character types and parse-handling behavior.  It stores\n    40→ * everything character- and handler-agnostic.\n    41→ *\n    42→ * ParserBase's most important field is the parser's token stream's\n    43→ * |TokenStreamAnyChars| component, for all tokenizing aspects that are\n    44→ * character-type-agnostic.  The character-type-sensitive components residing\n    45→ * in |TokenStreamSpecific| (see the comment near the top of TokenStream.h)\n    46→ * live elsewhere in this hierarchy.  These separate locations are the reason\n    47→ * for the |AnyCharsAccess| template parameter to |TokenStreamChars| and\n    48→ * |TokenStreamSpecific|.\n    49→ *\n    50→ * == PerHandlerParser<ParseHandler> → ParserBase ==\n    51→ *\n    52→ * Certain parsing behavior varies between full parsing and syntax-only parsing\n    53→ * but does not vary across source-text character types.  For example, the work\n    54→ * to \"create an arguments object for a function\" obviously varies between\n    55→ * syntax and full parsing but (because no source characters are examined) does\n    56→ * not vary by source text character type.  Such functionality is implemented\n    57→ * through functions in PerHandlerParser.\n    58→ *\n    59→ * Functionality only used by syntax parsing or full parsing doesn't live here:\n    60→ * it should be implemented in the appropriate Parser<ParseHandler> (described\n    61→ * further below).\n    62→ *\n    63→ * == GeneralParser<ParseHandler, Unit> → PerHandlerParser<ParseHandler> ==\n    64→ *\n    65→ * Most parsing behavior varies across the character-type axis (and possibly\n    66→ * along the full/syntax axis).  For example:\n    67→ *\n    68→ *   * Parsing ECMAScript's Expression production, implemented by\n    69→ *     GeneralParser::expr, varies in this manner: different types are used to\n    70→ *     represent nodes in full and syntax parsing (ParseNode* versus an enum),\n    71→ *     and reading the tokens comprising the expression requires inspecting\n    72→ *     individual characters (necessarily dependent upon character type).\n    73→ *   * Reporting an error or warning does not depend on the full/syntax parsing\n    74→ *     distinction.  But error reports and warnings include a line of context\n    75→ *     (or a slice of one), for pointing out where a mistake was made.\n    76→ *     Computing such line of context requires inspecting the source text to\n    77→ *     make that line/slice of context, which requires knowing the source text\n    78→ *     character type.\n    79→ *\n    80→ * Such functionality, implemented using identical function code across these\n    81→ * axes, should live in GeneralParser.\n    82→ *\n    83→ * GeneralParser's most important field is the parser's token stream's\n    84→ * |TokenStreamSpecific| component, for all aspects of tokenizing that (contra\n    85→ * |TokenStreamAnyChars| in ParserBase above) are character-type-sensitive.  As\n    86→ * noted above, this field's existence separate from that in ParserBase\n    87→ * motivates the |AnyCharsAccess| template parameters on various token stream\n    88→ * classes.\n    89→ *\n    90→ * Everything in PerHandlerParser *could* be folded into GeneralParser (below)\n    91→ * if desired.  We don't fold in this manner because all such functions would\n    92→ * be instantiated once per Unit -- but if exactly equivalent code would be\n    93→ * generated (because PerHandlerParser functions have no awareness of Unit),\n    94→ * it's risky to *depend* upon the compiler coalescing the instantiations into\n    95→ * one in the final binary.  PerHandlerParser guarantees no duplication.\n    96→ *\n    97→ * == Parser<ParseHandler, Unit> final → GeneralParser<ParseHandler, Unit> ==\n    98→ *\n    99→ * The final (pun intended) axis of complexity lies in Parser.\n   100→ *\n   101→ * Some functionality depends on character type, yet also is defined in\n   102→ * significantly different form in full and syntax parsing.  For example,\n   103→ * attempting to parse the source text of a module will do so in full parsing\n   104→ * but immediately fail in syntax parsing -- so the former is a mess'o'code\n   105→ * while the latter is effectively |return null();|.  Such functionality is\n   106→ * defined in Parser<SyntaxParseHandler or FullParseHandler, Unit> as\n   107→ * appropriate.\n   108→ *\n   109→ * There's a crucial distinction between GeneralParser and Parser, that\n   110→ * explains why both must exist (despite taking exactly the same template\n   111→ * parameters, and despite GeneralParser and Parser existing in a one-to-one\n   112→ * relationship).  GeneralParser is one unspecialized template class:\n   113→ *\n   114→ *   template<class ParseHandler, typename Unit>\n   115→ *   class GeneralParser : ...\n   116→ *   {\n   117→ *     ...parsing functions...\n   118→ *   };\n   119→ *\n   120→ * but Parser is one undefined template class with two separate\n   121→ * specializations:\n   122→ *\n   123→ *   // Declare, but do not define.\n   124→ *   template<class ParseHandler, typename Unit> class Parser;\n   125→ *\n   126→ *   // Define a syntax-parsing specialization.\n   127→ *   template<typename Unit>\n   128→ *   class Parser<SyntaxParseHandler, Unit> final\n   129→ *     : public GeneralParser<SyntaxParseHandler, Unit>\n   130→ *   {\n   131→ *     ...parsing functions...\n   132→ *   };\n   133→ *\n   134→ *   // Define a full-parsing specialization.\n   135→ *   template<typename Unit>\n   136→ *   class Parser<SyntaxParseHandler, Unit> final\n   137→ *     : public GeneralParser<SyntaxParseHandler, Unit>\n   138→ *   {\n   139→ *     ...parsing functions...\n   140→ *   };\n   141→ *\n   142→ * This odd distinction is necessary because C++ unfortunately doesn't allow\n   143→ * partial function specialization:\n   144→ *\n   145→ *   // BAD: You can only specialize a template function if you specify *every*\n   146→ *   //      template parameter, i.e. ParseHandler *and* Unit.\n   147→ *   template<typename Unit>\n   148→ *   void\n   149→ *   GeneralParser<SyntaxParseHandler, Unit>::foo() {}\n   150→ *\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. If a copy of the MPL was not distributed with this\n * file, You can obtain one at http://mozilla.org/MPL/2.0/. */\n\n/* JS parser. */\n\n#ifndef frontend_Parser_h\n#define frontend_Parser_h\n\n/*\n * [SMDOC] JS Parser\n *\n * JS parsers capable of generating ASTs from source text.\n *\n * A parser embeds token stream information, then gets and matches tokens to\n * generate a syntax tree that, if desired, BytecodeEmitter will use to compile\n * bytecode.\n *\n * Like token streams (see the comment near the top of TokenStream.h), parser\n * classes are heavily templatized -- along the token stream's character-type\n * axis, and also along a full-parse/syntax-parse axis.  Certain limitations of\n * C++ (primarily the inability to partially specialize function templates),\n * plus the desire to minimize compiled code size in duplicate function\n * template instantiations wherever possible, mean that Parser exhibits much of\n * the same unholy template/inheritance complexity as token streams.\n *\n * == ParserSharedBase ==\n *\n * ParserSharedBase is the base class for both regular JS and BinAST parsing.\n * This class contains common fields and methods between both parsers. There is\n * currently no BinAST parser here so this can potentially be merged into the\n * ParserBase type below.\n *\n * == ParserBase → ParserSharedBase, ErrorReportMixin ==\n *\n * ParserBase is the base class for regular JS parser, shared by all regular JS\n * parsers of all character types and parse-handling behavior.  It stores\n * everything character- and handler-agnostic.\n *\n * ParserBase's most important field is the parser's token stream's\n * |TokenStreamAnyChars| component, for all tokenizing aspects that are\n * character-type-agnostic.  The character-type-sensitive components residing\n * in |TokenStreamSpecific| (see the comment near the top of TokenStream.h)\n * live elsewhere in this hierarchy.  These separate locations are the reason\n * for the |AnyCharsAccess| template parameter to |TokenStreamChars| and\n * |TokenStreamSpecific|.\n *\n * == PerHandlerParser<ParseHandler> → ParserBase ==\n *\n * Certain parsing behavior varies between full parsing and syntax-only parsing\n * but does not vary across source-text character types.  For example, the work\n * to \"create an arguments object for a function\" obviously varies between\n * syntax and full parsing but (because no source characters are examined) does\n * not vary by source text character type.  Such functionality is implemented\n * through functions in PerHandlerParser.\n *\n * Functionality only used by syntax parsing or full parsing doesn't live here:\n * it should be implemented in the appropriate Parser<ParseHandler> (described\n * further below).\n *\n * == GeneralParser<ParseHandler, Unit> → PerHandlerParser<ParseHandler> ==\n *\n * Most parsing behavior varies across the character-type axis (and possibly\n * along the full/syntax axis).  For example:\n *\n *   * Parsing ECMAScript's Expression production, implemented by\n *     GeneralParser::expr, varies in this manner: different types are used to\n *     represent nodes in full and syntax parsing (ParseNode* versus an enum),\n *     and reading the tokens comprising the expression requires inspecting\n *     individual characters (necessarily dependent upon character type).\n *   * Reporting an error or warning does not depend on the full/syntax parsing\n *     distinction.  But error reports and warnings include a line of context\n *     (or a slice of one), for pointing out where a mistake was made.\n *     Computing such line of context requires inspecting the source text to\n *     make that line/slice of context, which requires knowing the source text\n *     character type.\n *\n * Such functionality, implemented using identical function code across these\n * axes, should live in GeneralParser.\n *\n * GeneralParser's most important field is the parser's token stream's\n * |TokenStreamSpecific| component, for all aspects of tokenizing that (contra\n * |TokenStreamAnyChars| in ParserBase above) are character-type-sensitive.  As\n * noted above, this field's existence separate from that in ParserBase\n * motivates the |AnyCharsAccess| template parameters on various token stream\n * classes.\n *\n * Everything in PerHandlerParser *could* be folded into GeneralParser (below)\n * if desired.  We don't fold in this manner because all such functions would\n * be instantiated once per Unit -- but if exactly equivalent code would be\n * generated (because PerHandlerParser functions have no awareness of Unit),\n * it's risky to *depend* upon the compiler coalescing the instantiations into\n * one in the final binary.  PerHandlerParser guarantees no duplication.\n *\n * == Parser<ParseHandler, Unit> final → GeneralParser<ParseHandler, Unit> ==\n *\n * The final (pun intended) axis of complexity lies in Parser.\n *\n * Some functionality depends on character type, yet also is defined in\n * significantly different form in full and syntax parsing.  For example,\n * attempting to parse the source text of a module will do so in full parsing\n * but immediately fail in syntax parsing -- so the former is a mess'o'code\n * while the latter is effectively |return null();|.  Such functionality is\n * defined in Parser<SyntaxParseHandler or FullParseHandler, Unit> as\n * appropriate.\n *\n * There's a crucial distinction between GeneralParser and Parser, that\n * explains why both must exist (despite taking exactly the same template\n * parameters, and despite GeneralParser and Parser existing in a one-to-one\n * relationship).  GeneralParser is one unspecialized template class:\n *\n *   template<class ParseHandler, typename Unit>\n *   class GeneralParser : ...\n *   {\n *     ...parsing functions...\n *   };\n *\n * but Parser is one undefined template class with two separate\n * specializations:\n *\n *   // Declare, but do not define.\n *   template<class ParseHandler, typename Unit> class Parser;\n *\n *   // Define a syntax-parsing specialization.\n *   template<typename Unit>\n *   class Parser<SyntaxParseHandler, Unit> final\n *     : public GeneralParser<SyntaxParseHandler, Unit>\n *   {\n *     ...parsing functions...\n *   };\n *\n *   // Define a full-parsing specialization.\n *   template<typename Unit>\n *   class Parser<SyntaxParseHandler, Unit> final\n *     : public GeneralParser<SyntaxParseHandler, Unit>\n *   {\n *     ...parsing functions...\n *   };\n *\n * This odd distinction is necessary because C++ unfortunately doesn't allow\n * partial function specialization:\n *\n *   // BAD: You can only specialize a template function if you specify *every*\n *   //      template parameter, i.e. ParseHandler *and* Unit.\n *   template<typename Unit>\n *   void\n *   GeneralParser<SyntaxParseHandler, Unit>::foo() {}\n *","numLines":150,"startLine":1,"totalLines":1974}}}

This is information conceptually \"passed down\"\n    53→ * into parsing sub-nodes. This is like \"are we parsing strict code?\", and so\n    54→ * the parser can make decisions of how to parse based off that.\n    55→ *\n    56→ * Gathered-while-parsing information. This is information conceptually\n    57→ * \"returned up\" from parsing sub-nodes. This is like \"did we see a use strict\n    58→ * directive\"?\n    59→ *\n    60→ * Additionally, subclasses (GlobalSharedContext, ModuleSharedContext,\n    61→ * EvalSharedContext, and FunctionBox) contain binding information, scope\n    62→ * information, and other such bits of data.\n    63→ *\n    64→ * ParseContext.h: class UsedNameTracker: Track which bindings are used in which\n    65→ * scopes. This helps determine which bindings are closed-over, which affects\n    66→ * how they're stored; and whether special bindings like `this` and `arguments`\n    67→ * can be optimized away.\n    68→ *\n    69→ * ParseContext.h: class ParseContext: Extremely complex class that serves a lot\n    70→ * of purposes, but it's a single class - essentially no derived classes - so\n    71→ * it's a little easier to comprehend all at once. (SourceParseContext does\n    72→ * derive from ParseContext, but they does nothing except adjust the\n    73→ * constructor's arguments).\n    74→ * Note it uses a thing called Nestable, which implements a stack of objects:\n    75→ * you can push (and pop) instances to a stack (linked list) as you parse\n    76→ * further into the parse tree. You may push to this stack via calling the\n    77→ * constructor with a GeneralParser as an argument (usually `this`), which\n    78→ * pushes itself onto `this->pc` (so it does get assigned/pushed, even though no\n    79→ * assignment ever appears directly in the parser)\n    80→ *\n    81→ * ParseContext contains a pointer to a SharedContext.\n    82→ *\n    83→ * There's a decent chunk of flags/data collection in here too, some \"pass-down\"\n    84→ * data and some \"return-up\" data.\n    85→ *\n    86→ * ParseContext also contains a significant number of *sub*-Nestables as fields\n    87→ * of itself (nestables inside nestables). \"symbol\": \"JSScript::fullyInitialize\"\n    }\n  ],\n  \"text\": \"The Firefox SpiderMonkey JavaScript parsing to bytecode compilation pipeline follows this architecture:\\n\\n**1. Parser Entry Point (js/src/frontend/Parser.h, js/src/frontend/Parser.cpp):**\\nThe parser hierarchy consists of ParserBase (base class for all parsers), PerHandlerParser (behavior shared across parse-handling modes), GeneralParser (character-type and handler-aware parsing), and specialized Parser<ParseHandler, Unit> templates. The GeneralParser class contains the core parsing logic that reads tokens and creates the Abstract Syntax Tree (AST).\\n\\n**2. AST Node Types (js/src/frontend/ParseNode.h, js/src/jsast.tbl):**\\nThe AST is defined through the ParseNode class hierarchy in ParseNode.h, with node kinds enumerated using the FOR_EACH_PARSE_NODE_KIND macro. Each node type (EmptyStmt, ExpressionStmt, CommaExpr, PropertyDefinition, etc.) maps to a corresponding ParseNode subclass (NullaryNode, UnaryNode, BinaryNode, ListNode, etc.). The jsast.tbl file provides a table of all AST node kinds with their names.\\n\\n**3. Bytecode Emitter (js/src/frontend/BytecodeEmitter.h, js/src/frontend/BytecodeEmitter.cpp):**\\nThe BytecodeEmitter class receives the parsed AST and generates bytecode sequences and related stencil data. BytecodeEmitter walks the AST and emits JSOp instructions (defined in vm/Opcodes.h), managing scope information, variable bindings, and jump targets. The emitter generates both prologue code (variable instantiation) and main code sections.\\n\\n**4. Intermediate Representation - Stencil (js/src/frontend/Stencil.h, js/src/frontend/Stencil.cpp):**\\nStencils are the intermediate representation produced by parsing and bytecode emission. ScriptStencil contains bytecode and metadata for each script/function.
